falkland suckling haust drummond stirl earl hall crashaw rowley nash ford middleton the life of the poet \* \* \* \* geoffry chaucer it have be observe that man of eminence in all age and distinguish for the same excellence have generally have something in they life similar to each other the place of homer 's nativity have not be more variously conjecture or he parent more differently assign than we author 's leland who live nearest to chaucer 's time of all those who have write he life be commission by king henry viii to search all the library and religious house in england when those archive be preserve before they destruction be produce by the reformation or polydore virgil have consume such curious piece as would have contradict he frame and fabulous history he for some reason believe oxford or berkshire to have give birth to this great man but have not inform we what those reason be that induce he to believe so and at present there appear no other but that the seat of he family be in those country pitt positively assert without produce any authority to support it that woodstock be the place which opinion mr camden seem to hint at where he mention that town but it may be suspect that pitt have no other ground for the assertion than chaucer 's mention woodstock park in he work and have a house there but after all these different pretension he himself in the testament of love seem to point out the place of he nativity to be the city of london and tho ' mr camden mention the claim of woodstock he do not give much credit to it for speaking of spencer -lrb- who be uncontrovertedly bear in london -rrb- he call he fellow citizen to chaucer the descent of chaucer be as uncertain and unfixed by the critic as the place of he birth mr speight be of opinion that one richard chaucer be he father and that one elizabeth chaucer a nun of st helen 's in the second year of richard ii might have be he sister or of he kindred but this conjecture say urry -lrb- 1 -rrb- seem very improbable for this richard be a vintner living at the corner of kirton-lane and at he death leave he house tavern and stock to the church of st mary aldermary which in all probability he would not have do if he have have any son to possess he fortune nor be it very likely he could enjoy the family estate mention by leland in oxfordshire and at the same time follow such a occupation pitt assert that he father be a knight but tho ' there be no authority to support this assertion yet it be reasonable to suppose that he be something superior to a common employ we find one john chaucer attend upon edward iii and queen philippa in they expedition to flander and cologn who have the king 's protection to go over sea in the twelfth year of he reign it be highly probable that this gentleman be father to we geoffry and the supposition be strengthen by chaucer 's first application after leave the university and inn of law be to the court nor be it unlikely that the service of the father should recommend the son it be universally agree that he be bear in the second year of the reign of king edward iii ad 1328 he first study be in the university of cambridge and when about eighteen year of age he write he court of love but of what college he be be uncertain there be no account of he in the record of the university from cambridge he be remove to oxford in order to compleat he study and after a considerable stay there and a strict application to the public lecture of the university he become -lrb- say leland -rrb- a ready logician a smooth rhetorician a pleasant poet a great philosopher a ingenious mathematician and a holy divine that he be a great master in astronomy be plain by he discourse of the astrolabe that he be verse in hermetic philosophy -lrb- which prevail much at that time -rrb- appear by he tale of the chanon yeoman : he knowledge in divinity be evident from he parson 's tale and he philosophy from the testament of love thus qualify to make a figure in the world he leave he learn retirement and travel into france holland and other country where he spend some of he younger day upon he return he enter himself in the inner temple where he study the municipal law of the land but he have not long prosecute that dry study till he superior ability be take notice of by some person of distinction by whole patronage he then approach the splendor of the court the reign of edward iii be glorious and successful he be a discerning as well as a fortunate monarch he have a taste as well for erudition as for arm he be a encourager of man of wit and part and permit they to approach he without reserve at edward 's court nothing but gallantry and a round of pleasure prevail and how well qualify we poet be to shine in the soft circle whoever have read he work will be at no loss to determine but besides the advantage of he wit and learn he possess those of person in a very considerable degree he be then about the age of thirty of a fair beautiful complexion he lip red and full he size of a just medium and he air polish and graceful so that he unite whatever could claim the approbation of the great and charm the eye of the fair he have ability to record the valor of the one and celebrate the beauty of the other and be qualify by he genteel behavior to entertain both he become a finish courtier the first dignity to which we find he prefer be that of page to the king a place of so much honor and esteem at that time that richard ii leave particular legacy to he page when few other of he servant be take notice of in the forty-first year of edward iii he receive as a reward of he service a annuity of twenty mark per ann payable out of the exchequer which in those day be no inconsiderable pension in a year after he be advance to be of he majesty 's privy chamber and a very few month to be he shield bearer a title at that time -lrb- tho ' now extinct -rrb- of very great honor be always next the king 's person and generally upon signal victory reward with military honor we poet be thus eminent by he place contract friendship and procure the esteem of person of the first quality queen philippa the duke of lancaster and he duchess blanch shew particular honor to he and lady margaret the king 's daughter and the countess of pembroke give he they warmest patronage as a poet in he poem call the romaunt and the rose and troilus and creseide he give offence to some court lady by the looseness of he description which the lady margaret resent and oblige he to atone for it by he legend of good woman a piece as chaste as the other be luxuriously amorous and under the name of the daisy he veil lady margaret whom of all he patron he most esteemed thus love and honor he younger year be dedicate to pleasure and the court by the recommendation of the dutchess blanch he marry one philippa rouet sister to the guardianess of she grace 's child who be a native of hainault : he be then about thirty year of age and be fix by marriage the king begin to employ he in more public and advantageous post in the forty-sixth year of he majesty 's reign chaucer be send to venice in commission with other to treat with the doge and senate of genoa about affair of great importance to we state the duke of lancaster whose favorite passion be ambition which demand the assistance of learn man engage warmly in we poet 's interest besides the duke be remarkably fond of lady catherine swynford he wife 's sister who be then guardianess to he child and whom he afterwards make he wife thus be he doubly attach to chaucer and with the vary fortune of the duke of lancaster we find he rise or fall much about this time for he successful negociation at genoa the king grant to he by letter patent by the title of armiger noster one pitcher of wine daily in the port of london and soon after make he comptroller of the custom with this particular proviso that he should personally execute the office and write the account relate to it with he own hand but as he be advance to higher place of trust so he become more entangled in the affair of state the consequence of which prove very prejudicial to he the duke of lancaster have be the chief instrument of raise he to dignity expect the fruit of those favor in a ready compliance with he in all he design that prince be certainly one of the proudest and most ambitious man of he time nor could he patiently bear the name of a subject even to he father nothing but absolute power and the title of king could satisfy he upon the death of he elder brother edward the black prince he fix a eye upon the english crown and seem to stretch out a impatient hand to reach it in this view he seek by all means possible to secure he interest against the decease of the old king and be afraid of the opposition of the clergy who be always strenuous against a irregular succession he embrace the opinion and espouse the interest of wickliff who now appear at oxford and be a man of very great ability and much esteem at court draw over to he party great number as well fashionable as low people in this confusion the duke of lancaster endeavor all he could to shake the power of the clergy and to procure votary amongst the lead popular man chaucer have no small hand in promote these proceedings both by he public interest and writings towards the close of edward 's reign he be very active in the intrigue of the court party and so recommend himself to the prince successor that upon he ascend the throne he confirm to he by the title of dilectus armiger noster the grant make by the late king of twenty mark per annum and at the same time confirm the other grant of the late king for a pitcher of wine to be deliver he daily in the port of london in less than two year after this we find we poet so reduce in he cirumstance -lrb- but by what mean be unknown -rrb- that the king in order to screen he from he creditor take he under he protection and allow he still to enjoy he former grant the duke of lancaster whose restless ambition ever excite he to disturb the state engage now with all the interest of which he be master to promote himself to the crown the opinion of wickliff gain ground and so great a commotion now prevail amongst the clergy that the king perceive the state in danger and be willing to support the clerical interest suffer the archbishop of canterbury to summon wickliff to appear before he whose interest after this arraignment very much decayed -lrb- 2 -rrb- the king who be devote to he pleasure resign himself to some young courtier who hate the duke of lancaster and cause a fryar to accuse he of a attempt to kill the king but before he have a opportunity of make out the charge against he the fryar be murder in a cruel and barbarous manner by lord john holland to whose care he have be commit this lord john holland call lord huntingdon and duke of exeter be half brother to the king and have marry elizabeth daughter of the duke of lancaster he be a great patron of chaucer and much respect by he with the duke of lancaster 's interest chaucer 's also sink he patron be unable to support he he could no longer struggle against opposite party or maintain he post of honor the duke pass over sea he friend feel all the malice of a enraged court which induce they to call in a number of the populace to assist they of which we poet be a zealous promoter one john of northampton a late lord mayor of london be at the head of these disturbance which do not long continue for upon beheading one of the rioter and northampton 's be take into custody the commotion subside strict search be make after chaucer who escape into hainault afterwards he go to france and find the king resolute to get he into he hand he flee from thence to zealand several accomplice in this affair be with he whom he support in they exile while the chief ringleader -lrb- except northampton who be condemn at read upon the evidence of he clerk -rrb- have restore themselves to court favor by acknowledge they crime and now forget the integrity and resolution of chaucer who suffer exile to secure they secret and so monstrously ungrateful be they that they wish he death and by keep supplies of money from he endeavor to effect it -- while he expend he fortune in remove from place to place and in support he fellow exile so far from receive any assistance from england he apartment be let and the money receive for rent be never acccount for to he nor could he recover any from those who owe it he they be of opinion it be impossible for he ever to return to he own country the government still pursue they resentment against he and he friend they be oblige to leave zealand and chaucer be unable to bear longer the calamity of poverty and exile and finding no security wherever he flee choose rather to throw himself upon the law of he country than perish abroad by hunger and oppression he have not long return till he be arrest by order of the king and confine in the tower of london the court sometimes flatter he with the return of the royal favor if he would impeach he accomplice and sometimes threaten he with immediate destruction they threat and promise he along while disregard but recollect the ingratitude of he old friend and the misery he have already suffer he at last make a confession and accord to the custom of trial at that time offer to prove the truth of it by combat what the consequence of this discovery be to he accomplice be uncertain it no doubt expose he to they resentment and procure he the name of a traytor but the king who regard he as one beloved by he grandfather be please to pardon he thus fall from a heighth of greatness we poet retire to bemoan the fickleness of fortune and then write he testament of love in which be many pathetic exclamation concern the vicissitude of human thing which he then bitterly experienced but as he have formerly be the favorite of fortune when dignity be multiply thick upon he so he misery now succeed with a equal swiftness he be not only discard by he majesty unpensioned and abandon but he lose the favor of the duke of lancaster as the influence of he wife 's sister with that prince be now much lessen the duke be deject with the trouble in which he be involve begin to reflect on he vicious course of life and particularly he keep that lady as he concubine which produce a resolution of put she out of he house and he make a vow to that purpose chaucer thus reduce and weary of the perpetual turmoil at court retire to woodstock to enjoy a studious quiet where he write he excellent treatise of the astrolabe but notwithstanding the severe treatment of the government he still retain he loyalty and strictly enjoin he son to pray for the king as the pious resolution of some people be often the consequence of a present evil so at the return of prosperity they be soon dissipate this prove the case with the duke of lancaster : he party again gather strength he interest begin to rise upon which he take again he mistress to he bosom and not content with heaping favor honor and title upon she he make she he wife procure a act of parliament to legitimate she child which give great offence to the duchess of gloucester the countess of derby and arundel as she then be entitle to take place of they with she interest chaucer 's also return and after a long and bitter storm the sun begin to shine upon he with a evening ray for at the sixty-fifth year of he age the king grant to he by the title of delectus armiger noster a annuity of twenty mark per annum during he life as a compensation for the former pension he needy circumstance oblige he to part with but however sufficient that might be for present support yet as he be encumber with debt he dare not appear publickly till he majesty again grant he he royal protection to screen he from the persecution of he creditor he also restore to he he grant of a pitcher of wine daily and a pipe annually to be deliver to he by he son thoma who that year possess the office of chief butler to the king now that i have mention he son it will not be improper to take a view of we author 's domestical affair at least as far as we be enable by material that have descend to we time thoma he eldest son be marry to one of the greatest fortune in england maud daughter and heir of sir john burgheershe knight of the garter and dr henry burghurshe bishop of lincoln chancellor and treasurer of england mr speight say this lady be give he in marriage by edward iii in return of he service perform in he embassy in france he second son lewi be bear in 1381 for when he father write the treatise of the astrolabe he be ten year old he be then a student in merton college in oxford and pupil to nicholas strade but there be no further account of he thoma who now enjoy the office of chief butler to he majesty have the same place confirm to he for life by letter patent to king henry iv and continue by henry vi in the 2d year of henry iv we find he speaker of the house of common sheriff of oxfordshire and berkshire and constable of wallingford castle and knaresborough castle during life in the 6th year of the same prince he be send ambassador to france in the 9th of the same reign the common present he they speaker as they do likewise in the 11th year soon after this queen jane grant to he for he good service the manor of woodstock hannerborough and wotten during life and in the 13th year he be again present speaker as he be in the 2d of henry v and much about that time he be send by the king to treat of a marriage with catherine daughter to the duke of burgundy he be send again ambassador to france and pass thro ' a great many public station mr stebbing say that he be knight but we find no such title give he in any record he die at ewelm the chief place of he residence in the year 1434 by he wife maud he have one daughter name alice who be thrice marry first to sir john philip and afterwards to thoma montacute earl of salisbury : she third husband be the famous william de la pole duke of suffolk who lose he head by the fury of the yorkist who dread he influence in the opposite party tho ' he stand proscribe by the parliament of henry vi for misguide that easy prince they son john have three son the second of whom edmund forfeit he life to the crown for treason against henry vii by which mean the estate which chaucer 's family possess come to the crown but to return to we poet : by means of the duke of lancaster 's marriage with he sister in law he again grow to a considerable share of wealth but be now about seventy year of age and fatigue with a tedious view of hurry greatness he quit the stage of grandeur where he have act so considerable a part with varied success and retire to dunnigton castle -lrb- 3 -rrb- near newbury to reflect at leisure upon past transaction in the still retreat of contemplation in this retirement do he spend he few remain year universally love and honor he be familiar with all man of learn in he time and contract friendship with person of the greatest eminence as well in literature as politics gower occleve lidgate wickliffe be great admirer and particular friend of chaucer besides he be well acquaint with foreign poet particularly franci petrarch the famous italian poet and refiner of the language a revolution in england soon after this happen in which we find chaucer but little concerned he make no mean compliment to henry iv but gower he cotemporary though then very old flatter the reign prince and insult the memory of he murder sovereign all act of parliament and grant in the last reign be annul chaucer again repair to court to get fresh grant but bend with age and weakness tho ' he be successful in he request the fatigue of attendance so overcome he that death prevent he enjoy he new possession he die the 25th of October in the year 1400 in the second of henry iv in the 72d of he age and bear the shock of death with the same fortitude and resignation with which he have undergo a variety of pressure and vicissitude of fortune dryden say he be poet laureat to three king but urry be of opinion that dryden must be mistake as among all he work not one court poem be to be find and selden observe that he could find no poet honor with that title in england before the reign of edward iv to whom one john kaye dedicate the siege of rhode in prose by the title of he humble poet laureat i can not better display the character of this great man than in the follow word of urry as to he temper say he he have a mixture of the gay the modest and the grave he reading be deep and extensive he judgment sound and discerning he be communicative of he knowledge and ready to correct or pass over the fault of he cotemporary writer he know how to judge of and excuse the slip of weaker capacity and pity rather than expose the ignorance of that age in one word he be a great scholar a pleasant wit a candid critic a sociable companion a stedfast friend a great philosopher a temperate oeconomist and a pious christian as to he genius as a poet dryden -lrb- than whom a higher authority can not be produce -rrb- speaking of homer and virgil positively assert that we author exceed the latter and stand in competition with the former he language how unintelligible soever it may seem be almost as modern as any of he cotemporary or of those who follow he at the distance of 50 or 60 year as harding skelton and other and in some place it be so smooth and beautiful that dryden would not attempt to alter it i shall now give some account of he work in the order in which they be write so far as can be collect from they and subjoin a specimen of he poetry of which profession as he may justly be call the morning star so as we descend into later time we may see the progress of poetry in england from its great original chaucer to its full blaze and work then and so highly value by the people of taste that they be except out of the prohibition of that act the pardoner prologue lording quoth he in chirch when i preche i paine mee to have a have a hauteine speche and ring it out as round as doth a bell for i can all by rote that i tell my teme be always one and ever be -lrb- radix omnium malorum est cupidita -rrb- first i pronounce fro whence i come and then my bill i shew all and some : we liege -- lord seal on my patent that shew i first my body to warrent that no man be so bold priest ne clerk I to disturb of christ 's holy werke and after that i tell forth my tale of bull of pope and of cardinale of patriarke and of bishop i shew and in latin i speake wordes a few to faver with my predication and for to stere man to devotion then shew i forth my long christall stone ycramm full of clout and of bone relicke they be as be they echone then have i in latin a shoder-bone which that be of a holy jewe shepe good man fay take of my word kepe if this bone be washen in any well if cow or calfe shepe or oxe swell that any worm have eat or hem strong take water of this well and wash he tong and it be hole a-non : and furthermore of pocke and scab and every sore shall shepe be hole that of this well drinketh a draught : take keep of that i tell if that the good man that beast oweth woll every day ere the cocke croweth fasting drink of this well a draught -lrb- as thilk holy jew we elder teach -rrb- he beast and he store shall multiplie : and sir also it healeth jealousie for though a man be fall in jealous rage let make with this water he potage and never shall he more he wife mistrist thughe in sooth the defaut by she wist : all have she take priest two or three here be a mittaine eke that ye may see he that have he hand well put in this mittaine he shall have multiply of he graine when he have sowen be it wheat or ote so that he offer good pen or grote those who would prefer the thought of this father of english this castle -rrb- \* \* \* \* \* langland it have be dispute amongst the critic whether this poet precede or follow chaucer mr cooper author of the muse library be of opinion that he precede chaucer and observe that in more place than one that great poet seem to copy langland but i be rather incline to believe that he be cotemporary with he which account for she observation and my conjecture be strengthen by the consideration of he stile which be equally unmusical and obsolete with chaucer 's and tho ' dryden have tell we that chaucer exceed those who follow he at 50 or 60 year distance in point of smoothness yet with great submission to he judgment i think there be some alteration even in skelton and harding which will appear to the reader to the best advantage by a quotation of langland 's family we have no account selden in he note on draiton 's poly olbion quote he with honor but he be entirely neglect by philip and winstanly tho ' he seem to have be a man of great genius : besides chaucer few poet in that or the subsequent age have more real inspiration or poetical enthusiasm in they composition one can not read the work of this author or chaucer without lament the unhappiness of a fluctuating language that bury in its ruin even genius itself for like edifice of sand every breath of time deface it and if the form remain the beauty be lose the piece from which i shall quote a few line be a work of great length and labor of the allegoric kind it be animated with a lively and luxurious imagination point with a variety of pungent satire and dignified with many excellent lesson of morality but as to the conduct of the whole it do not appear to be of a piece every vision seem a distinct rhapsody and do not carry on either one single action or a series of many but we ought rather to wonder at its beauty than cavil at its defect and if the poetical design be break the moral be entire which be uniformly the advancement of piety and reformation of the roman clergy the piece before we be entitle the vision of pier the plowman and i shall quote that particular part which seem to have furnish a hint to milton in he paradise lose b 2 1 475 kinde conscience triumphant death he dart shake p l b xi 1 477 \* \* \* \* \* sir john gower flourish in the reign of edward iii and richard ii he be cotemporary with chaucer and much esteemed and honor by he as family at sittenham in yorkshire he receive he education in london and study the law but be possess of a great fortune he dedicate himself more to pleasure and poetry than the bar tho ' he seem not to be son to sir john more knight and one of the justice of the king 's - bench a man hold in the highest esteem at that time for he knowledge in the law and he integrity in the administration of justice it be object by the enemy of sir thoma that he birth be obscure and he family mean but far otherwise be the real case judge more bear arm from he birth have he coat of arm quarter which prove he have obscure that she could not well discern it and indeed she afterwards suffer a untimely delivery of one of they : the face of the other she behold shine most gloriously by which the future fame of sir belief in miracle and vision there be no great stress to be lay upon it lady more might perhaps communicate this vision to she son and he have embrace the belief of it but it seem to have too little authority to deserve credit from posterity another miracle be relate by stapleton which be say to have happen in the infancy of more he nurse one day cross a river and she horse step into a deep place expose both she and the child to great danger she be more anxious for the safety of the child than she own throw he over a hedge into a field adjoining and escape likewise from the imminent danger when she come to take he up she find he quite unhurt and smile sweetly upon she he be put to the free-school in london call st anthony 's under the care of the famous nichola holt and when he have vivacity of wit he acquire the first character among the student and then give proof of a genius that would one day make a great blaze in the world when he be but eighteen year old such be the force of he understanding he write many epigram which be highly esteem by man of eminence as well abroad as at home beatus rhenanus in he epistle to bilibalus pitchemerus pass great encomium upon they as of erasmus be cheaply purchase by the malevolence of a thousand such critic as brixius about the same time of life he translate for he exercise one of lucian 's oration out of greek into latin which he call he first fruit of the greek tongue and add another oration of he own to answer that of lucian for as he have defend he who have slay a tyrant he oppose against it another with such forcible child and houshold affair upon what principle this observation be found i can not well conceive and wish sir thoma have give he reason why it be harder to be chaste in a married than single life this wife be a worldly mind woman have a very indifferent person be advance in year and possess no very agreeable temper much about this time he become obnoxious to henry vii for oppose he exaction upon of the state roper in he life of we author relate that upon a occasion in which king henry viii and the pope be party in a cause trye in the star chamber sir thoma most remarkably distinguish himself he grace bring with he the proud churchman have enter the house make a long speech for grant the subsidy and ask several of the member opinion concern it they be all so confound as not to be able to nullus upon which more reply aut tu es erasmus aut diabolus as at that time erasmus be strive to defend very impious proposition in order to put he antagonist 's strength to the proof when he live in the city of london as a justice of peace he use to attend the session at newgate there be then upon the bench a venerable old judge who be very severe against those who have they purse cut -lrb- as the phrase then be -rrb- and tell they that it be by they negligence that so many purse-cutter come before he sir thomas who be a great lover of a joke contrived to have this judge 's purse cut from he in the session house by a felon when the felon be arraign he tell the court that if he be permit to speak to one of the judge in private he could clear he innocence to they they indulge he in he accomplish the scheme sir thoma move the court that each of they should bestow some alms on a needy person who then stand falsly accuse and be a real object of compassion the motion be agree to and we with felony the judge begin to be angry we facetious author desire the felon to return he purse and advise the old man never to be so bitter against innocent man 's negligence when he himself could not keep he purse safe in that open assembly although he live a courtier and be much concern in business yet he never neglect he family at home but instruct he daughter in all useful learning and before nor ever since happen of a father be a judge and he son a chancellor at the same time every day as the chancellor go to the bench he kneel before he father and ask he blessing the people soon find the difference between the intolerable pride of wolsey and the gentleness and humility of more he permit every one to approach he without reserve he dispatch business with great assiduity and so clear the court of tedious suit that he more than once come to the bench and call for a cause there be none to try as no dignity could inspire he with pride so no application to the most important affair could divert he from sally of humor and a pleasantry of behavior it once happen that a beggar 's little dog which she have lose be present to lady more of which I be very fond but at last the beggar get notice where the dog be she come to complain to sir thoma as he be sit in he hall that he lady withhold she dog from she presently my lady be send for and the dog bring with she which he take in he hand cause he wife to stand at the upper end of the hall and the beggar at the other he then bad each of they call the dog which when they do the dog go presently to the beggar forsake my lady when he see this he bad my lady be contented for it be none of hers my lord chancellor then give the woman a piece of gold which would have buy ten such dog and bid she be careful of it for the future a friend of he have spend much time in compose a book and go to sir thoma to have he opinion of it he desire he to turn it into rhime which at the expence of many year labor he at last accomplished and come again to have he opinion : yea marry say he now it be somewhat now it be rhime but before it be neither rhime nor reason but fortune which have be long propitious to we author begin now to change side and try he as well with affliction as prosperity in both which character he behavior integrity and courage be irreproachable the amorous monarch king henry viii at last obtain but animated all she relation and the party incline to the protestant interest to persecute he with rigor not long after the divorce the council give authority for the publication of a book in which the reason why this divorce be grant be lay down a answer be soon publish with which sir thoma more be charge as the author of which report however he sufficiently clear himself in a letter to mr cromwel then secretary and a great favorite with king henry in the parliament hold in the year 1534 there be a oath frame call the oath of supremacy in which all english subject should renounce the pope 's authority and swear also to the succession of queen ann 's child and lady mary illegitimate this oath be give to all the clergy as well bishop as priest but no lay-man except sir thoma more be desire to take it he be summon to appear at lambeth before archbishop cranmer the lord chancellor audley mr secretary cromwel and the abbot of westminster appoint commissioner by the king to tender this oath more absolutely refuse to take it from a principle of conscience : and after various expostulation he be order into the custody of the abbot of westminster and soon after he be send to the spirit man and be not afraid to do thy office my neck be very short take heed therefore that thou strike not awry for save thy honesty thus by a honest but mistaken zeal fall sir thoma more a man of henry howard earl of surry be son of thoma duke of norfolk and elizabeth daughter of edward duke of buckingham the father of we author hold the highest place under king henry viii and have so faithfully and bravely serve he that the nobility grow jealous of he influence and by they unite effort produce he ruin after many excellent service in france he be constitute lord treasurer and make general of the king 's whole army design have to march against the scot : at the battle of flodden in which the scot be route and they sovereign slay the earl of surry remarkably distinguish himself he command under he father and as soon as the jealousy of the peer have fasten upon the one they take care that the other should not escape he be the first nobleman -lrb- say camden -rrb- that illustrate he high birth with the beauty of learn he be acknowledge by all to be the gallantest man the politest lover and the most compleat gentleman of he time he receive he education at windsor with a natural son of henry viii and become first eminent for he devotion to the beautiful geraldine maid of honor to queen catherine the first inspire he with poetry and that poetry have confer immortality on she : so transport be he with he passion that he make a tour to the most elegant court in europe to maintain she peerless beauty against all opposer and every where make good he challenge with honor in he way to florence he touch at the emperor 's court where he become acquaint with the learn cornelius agrippa so famous for magic who shew he the image of he geraldine in a glass sick weep on she bed and melting into devotion for the absence of she lord upon sight of this he write the follow passionate sonnet which for the smoothness of the verse the tenderness of expression and the heartfelt sentiment might do honor to the politest easiest most passionate poet in we own time all soul no earthly flesh why dost thou fade all gold no earthly dross why look ` st thou pale sickness how darest thou one so fair invade too base infirmity to work she bale heaven be distemper since she grieve pine never be dry these my sad plaintive line pearch thou my spirit on she silver breast and with they pain redouble musick beating let they toss thee to world where all toil rest where bliss be subject to no fear defeating she praise i tune whose tongue doth tune the sphere and get new muse in she hearer ear star fall to fetch fresh light from the rich eye she bright brow drive the fun to cloud beneath she hair reflex with red strake paint the skye sweet morn and evening dew flow from she breath : phoebe rule tide she my tear tide forth draw in she sick bed love fit and maketh law she dainty lip tinsel she silk-soft sheet she rose-crown would cheek eclipse my dazled sight o glass with too much joy my thought thou greet and yet thou shewest I day but by twilight i 'll kiss thee for the kindness i have feel she lip one kiss would into nectar melt from the emperor 's court he go to the city of florence the pride and glory of italy in which city he beauteous geraldine be bear and he have no rest till he find out the house of she nativity and be shewn the room where he charmer first draw air he be transport with extasy of joy he tongue overflow with she praise and winstanly say he eclipse the sun and moon with comparison of he geraldine and write another sonnet in praise of the chamber that be honor -lrb- as he say -rrb- with she radiant conception this sonnet be equally amorous and spirited with that already insert in the duke of florence 's court he publish a proud challenge against all comer whether christian turk canibal jew or saracen in defense of he mistress 's beauty this challenge be the better receive there as she whom he defend be bear in that city : the duke of florence however send for he and enquire of he fortune and the intent of he come to he court of which when the earl inform he he grant to all country whatever as well enemy and outlaw as friend and ally free access into he dominion unmolested till the trial be end in the course of he combat for he mistress he valor and skill in arm so engage the duke to he interest that he offer he the highest preferment if he would remain at he court this proposal he reject as he intend to proceed thro ' all the chief city in italy but he design be frustrate by letter send by king henry viii which command he speedy return into england in the year 1544 upon the expedition to boulogne in france he be make field marshal of the english army and after take that town be then knight of the garter he be in the beginning of September 1545 constitute the king 's lieutenant and captain-general of all he army within the town and county of boulogne -lrb- 1 -rrb- during he command there in 1546 hearing that a convoy of provision of the enemy be come to the fort at oultreaw he resolve to intercept it but the rhinegrave with four thousand lanskinet together with a considerable number of french under the de bieg make a obstinate defense the english be route sir edward poyning with diver other gentleman kill and the earl himself oblige to fly tho ' it appear by a letter to the king date January 8 1548 that this advantage cost the enemy a great number of man but the king be so highly displeased with this ill success that from that time he contract a prejudice against the earl and soon after remove he from he command and appoint the earl of hertford to succeed he upon which sir william page write to the earl of surry to advise he to procure some eminent post under the earl of hertford that he might not be unprovided in the town and field the earl be desirous in the mean time to regain he former favor with the king skirmish with the french and route they but soon after write over to the king 's council that as the enemy have cast much larger cannon than have be yet see with which they imagine they should soon demolish boulogne it deserve consideration whether the lower town should stand as not be defensible the council order he to return to england in order to represent he sentiment more fully upon those point and the earl of hertford be immediately send over in he room this exasperate the earl of surry occasion he to let fall some expression which favor of revenge and dislike to the king and a hatred of he councellor and be probably one cause of he ruin which soon after ensue the duke of norfolk who discover the grow power of the seymor and the influence they be likely to bear in the next reign be for make a alliance with they he therefore press he son to marry the earl of hertford 's daughter and the dutchess of richmond he own daughter to marry sir thoma seymor but neither of these match be effect and the seymor and howard then become open enemy the seymor fail not to inspire the king with a aversion to the norfolk-family whose power they dread and represent the ambitious view of the earl of surry but to return to he as a poet that celebrate antiquary john leland speaking of sir thoma wyat the elder call the earl ` the conscript enrol heir of the say sir thoma in he learning and other excellent quality ' the author of a treatise entitle ` the art of english poetry alledge that sir thoma wyat the elder and henry earl of surry be the two chieftain who have travel into italy and there taste the sweet and stately measure and stile of the italian poetry greatly polish we rude and homely manner of vulgar poetry from what it have be before and therefore may be justly call the reformer of we english poetry and stile ' we noble author add to learn wisdom fortitude munificence and affability yet all these excellency of character could not prevent he fall a sacrifice to the jealousy of the peer or as some say to the resentment of the king for he attempt to wed the princess mary and by these means to raise himself to the crown history be silent as to the reason why the gallantry he perform for geraldine do not issue in a marriage perhaps the reputation he acquire by arm might have enflame he soul with a love of glory and this conjecture seem the more probable as we find he ambition prompt he to make love to the princess from no other view but those of dominion he marry france daughter to john earl of oxford after whose death he address princess mary and he first marriage perhaps might be owe to a desire of strengthen he interest and advance he power in the realm the add some part of the royal arm to he own be also make a pretence against he but in this he be justify by the herald as he prove that a power of do so be grant by some preceeding monarch to he forefather upon the strength of these suspicion and surmise he and he father be commit to the tower of london the one by water the other by land so that they know not of each other 's apprehension the fifteenth day of January next follow he be arraign at guildhall where he be find guilty by twelve common juryman and receive judgment about nine day before the death of the king he lose he head on tower-hill and have not that monarch 's decease so soon ensue the fate of he father be likewise determine to have be the same with he son it be say when a courtier ask king henry why he be so zealous in take off surry i observe he say he a enterprize youth he spirit be too great to brook subjection and ` tho ' i can manage he yet no successor of mine will ever be able to do so for which reason i have dispatch he in my own time he be first inter in the chapel of the tower and afterwards in the reign of king jame he remains be remove to farmingam in suffolk by he second son henry earl of northampton with this epitaph henrico howardo thomæ secundus duci norfolciæ filio daughter of sir john bruge lord of london -lrb- 1 -rrb- he be bear at buckhurst in the parish of withiam in suffex and from he childhood be distinguish for wit and manly behavior : he be first of the university of oxford but take no degree there he go to cambridge and commence master of art he afterwards study the law in the inner-temple and become a barrister but he genius be too lively to be perform in the presence of queen elizabeth long before shakespear appear -lrb- 2 -rrb- on the stage by the gentleman of the inner-temple at whitehall the 18th of January 1561 which sir philip sidney thus characterise : it be full of stately speech and well founding phrase climb to the height of seneca 's stile and as full of notable morality which it doth most delightfully teach and so obtain the very end of poetry in the course of he study he be most delighted with the history of he own country and be likewise well acquaint with antient history he form a design of write the life of several great personage in verse of which we have a specimen in a book publish 1610 call the mirror of magistrate be a true chronicle history of the untimely fall of such unfortunate prince and man of note as have happen since the first entrance of brute into this island until he own time it appear by a preface of richard nicoll that the original plan of the mirror of magistrate be principally owe to he a nation to this day nor could have sink under the ruin of time but the courtier put a end to the poet and one can not help wish for the sake of we national reputation that he rise at court have be a of water which whether by strain in he study the night before in which he sit up till 11 o'clock or otherwise by they own maturity suddenly break and fall upon he brain produce he death to the unite the greatest industry and variety of genius : it be seldom find that the son of parnassus can devote themselves to public business or execute it with success i have already observe that the world have lose many excellent work which no doubt this cultivate genius would have accomplish have he be less involve in court-affair : but as he act in so public a sphere and discharge every office he poverty for tho ' it generally happen that poet have the greatest power in courtship as they can celebrate they mistress with more elegance than people of any other profession yet it very seldom author 's case exactly he make love to a widow name browning who possess a very good jointure but this lady be more in love with money than laurel with wealth than merit reject he suit which not a far from st alban 's about the year 1510 -lrb- 1 -rrb- he be a lawyer a historian and a poet he receive he education at the university of oxford but of what college he be wood himself have not be able to discover he remove from thence to lincoln ' - inn where by a diligent application to the law he make considerable progress in he profession and by the patronage of that great minister cromwell earl of essex who be himself a man of astonishing ability he soon make a figure at the bar he be the menial servant of king henry viii -lrb- 2 -rrb- and discharge he trust both in time of war and peace with great honor and gallantry and share that monarch 's favor in a very considerable degree who make he a grant in he own country as a evidence of he affection for he this grant of the king 's happen in the year 1535 and yet in seven year afterwards either thro ' want of economy or by a boundless confidence in he friend he reduce he affair to a very indifferent situation which perhaps might be the reason why he procure himself to be choose member for the borough of plymouth in the county of devon -lrb- 3 -rrb- in the parliament summon the thirty-third year of that king 's reign during the session he have the misfortune to be arrest by a officer belong to the sheriff of london and carry to the counter then in bread-street no sooner have the house of common get notice of this insult offer to one of they member than they immediately enact a settle rule which from that accident take place with respect to privilege and ever since that time the member of the house have be exempt from arrest for debt he majesty likewise resent the affront offer to he servant and with the concurrence of the parliament proceed very severely against the sheriff hollinsh in he chronicle vol 2 p 955 give a very full account of it sir thoma moil knight then speaker of the house give a special order to the serjeant of the parliament to repair to the compter and there demand the delivery of the prisoner but notwithstanding this high authority the officer in the city refuse to obey the command and after many altercation they absolutely resist the serjeant upon which a fray ensue within the compter-gate between ferrar and the officer not without mutual hurt so that the serjeant be drive to defend himself with he mace of arm and have the crown of it break with ward off a stroke the sheriff of london so far from appease foment the quarrel and with insolent language refuse to deliver they prisoner : upon which the serjeant thus abuse return to the house and related what have happen this circumstance so exasperate the burgess that they all rise and go into the upper house and declare they would transact no more business till they member be restore to they they then command they serjeant again to go to the compter with he mace and make a second demand by they authority -- the sheriff hear that the upper house hide concerned themselves in it and be afraid of they resentment restore the prisoner before the serjeant have time to return to the compter but this do not satisfy the burgess they summon the sheriff before they together with one white who in contempt of they dignity have take out a writ against ferrar and as a punishment for they insolence they be send to the tower and ever since that period the power and privilege of the common have be on the increase ferrar continue in high favor with henry during the remainder of he reign and seem to have stand upon good term with somerset lord protector in the beginning of edward vi since it appear that he attend the protector in quality of one of the commissioner of the army in he expedition into scotland in 1548 -lrb- 4 -rrb- which perhaps might be owe to he be about the person of prince edward in he father 's life-time another instance of this happen about four year afterwards at a very critical juncture for when the unfortunate duke of somerset lay under sentence of death and it be observe that the people murmur and often give testimony of discontent and that the king himself be very uneasy those about he study every method to quiet and amuse the one to entertain and divert the other -lrb- 5 -rrb- in order to this at the entrance of christmas holiday mr ferrar be proclaimed lord misrule that be a kind of prince of sport and pastime which office he discharge for twelve day together at greenwich with great magnificence and address and entirely to the king 's satisfaction in this character attend by the politest part of the court he make a excursion to london where he be splendidly entertain by the lord mayor and when he take he leave he have present give he in token of respect but notwithstanding he make so great figure in the diversion at court yet he be no idle spectator of political affair and maintain he reputation with the learn world he write the reign of queen mary which tho ' publish in the name of richard grafton in he chronicle yet be certainly the performance of ferrar accord to the annals of stow p 632 whose authority in this case be very high we author be a historian a lawyer and a politician even in he poetry as appear from these piece of he which be insert in the mirror of magistrate and which be not inferior to any other that have find a place there -lrb- 6 -rrb- in the early part of he life he write some tract on he own profession change he situation or be force to do so that he continue to preserve the good opinion of different party but this be a happiness which ferrar enjoy he be consult by the learn as a candid critic george ferrar lately decease -lrb- 7 -rrb- none of we author deliver any thing as to mr ferrar 's religion but it be highly probable that he be a zealous protestant : not from he accept grant of abbey-land for that be but a precarious proof but from he come into the world under the protection of thoma lord cromwell who be certainly persuade of the truth of the protestant religion have this occasion to mention thoma lord cromwell the famous earl of essex who be we author 's warmest patron i be persuade my reader will forgive I a digression which will open to they the noblest instance of gratitude and honor in that worthy nobleman that ever adorn the page of a historian and which have be tell with rapture by all who have writ of the time particularly by dr burnet in he history of the reformation and fox in he martyrology -- thoma lord cromwell be the son of a blacksmith at putney and be a soldier under the duke of bourbon at the sack of rome in the year 1527 while he be abroad in a military character in a very low station he fall sick and be unable to follow the army he be observe one day by a italian merchant to walk very pensive and have all the appearance of penury and wretchedness : the merchant enquire of he the place of he birth and fortune and upon converse with cromwell be so well pleased with the account he give of himself that he supply he with money and credit to carry he to england cromwell afterwards make the most rapid progress in state-preferment ever know honor be multiply thick upon he and he come to have the dispensing of he sovereign 's bounty it happen that this italian merchant 's circumstance decay and he come to england to sollicit the payment of some debt due to he by he correspondent who find he necessitous be dispose to put he off and take the advantage of he want to avoid payment this not a little embarrassed the foreigner who be now in a situation forlorn enough as providence would have it lord cromwell then earl of essex ride to court see this merchant walk with a dejected countenance which put he in mind of he former situation he immediately order one of he attendant to desire the merchant to come to he house he lordship ask the merchant whether he know he he answer no : cromwell then relate the circumstance of the merchant 's relieve a certain englishman and ask if he remember it the merchant answer that he have always make it he business to do good but do not remember that circumstance -- he lordship then enquire the reason of he come to england and upon the merchant 's tell he he story he so interested himself as soon to procure the payment of all he debt -- cromwell then inform the merchant that he be himself the person he have thus relieved and for every ducat which the merchant have give he he return to the value of a hundred tell he that this be the payment of he debt he then make he a munificent present and ask he whether he choose to settle in england or return to he own country the foreigner choose the latter and return to spend the remainder of he day in competence and quiet after have experience in lord essex as high a instance of generosity and gratitude as perhaps ever be know this noble act of he lordship employ say burnet the pen of the belt writer at that time in panegyric on so great a behavior the finest poet praise he he most violent enemy could not help admire he and latest posterity shall hold the name of he in veneration who be capable of so generous a act of honor but to return to ferrar in we author 's history of the reign of queen mary tho ' he shew himself a great admirer of the personal virtue of that princess and a very discerning and able historian yet it be every where evident that he be attach to the protestant interest but more especially and to britain be the son of sir henry sidney knight of the garter and three time lord deputy of ireland and of lady mary dudley daughter to the duke of northumberland and nephew to that great favorite robert earl of leicester oxford have the honor of he education under the tuition of dr thoma thornton canon of christ church at the university he remain till he be 17 year of age and in June 1572 set out on he travels on the 24th of August follow when the massacre fall out at paris he be then there -lrb- 1 -rrb- and with other englishman take shelter in sir franci walsingham 's house she majesty 's ambassador at that court when this storm subside he depart from paris go through lorrain and by strasburgh and heydelburgh to francfort in September or October follow where he settle for some time and be entertain agent for the duke of saxony at he return she majesty be one of the first who distinguish he great ability and as proud of so rich a treasure she send he ambassador to rodolph the emperor to condole he on the death of maximilian and also to other prince of germany the next year 1577 he go to the court of that gallant prince don john de austria viceroy in the low country for the king of spain don john be the proudest man in he time haughty and imperious in he behavior and always use the foreign ambassador who come to he court with unsufferable insolence and superiority : at first he pay but little respect to sidney on account of he youth and seeming inexperience but have have occasion to hear he talk and give some account of the manners of every court where he have be he be so strike with he vivacity the propriety of he observation and the lustre of he part that he ever afterwards use he with familiarity and pay he more respect in he private character than he do to any ambassador from whatever court some year after this wood observe that in a book call cabalum he set forth he reason why the marriage of the queen with the duke of anjou be disadvantageous to the nation this address be write at the desire of the earl of leicester he uncle upon which a quarrel happen between he and the earl of oxford which perhaps occasion he retirement from court for two year when he write that renowned romance call arcadium we find he again in high favor when the treaty of marriage be renew he be engage with sir fulk greville in tilt for the diversion of the court and at the departure of the duke of anjou from england he attend he to antwerp -lrb- 2 -rrb- on the 8th of January 1582 he receive the honor of knighthood from the queen and in the beginning of the year 1585 he design a expedition with sir franci drake into america but be hinder by the queen who think the court would be deficient without he he be make governor of flushing -lrb- about that time deliver to the queen for one of the cautionary-town -rrb- and general of the horse in both these place of important trust he behavior in point of prudence and valor be irreproachable and gain additional honor to he country especially when in July 1586 he surprize axil and preserve the life and reputation of the english army at the enterprise of gravelin about that time he be in election for the crown of poland but the queen refuse to promote this he glorious advancement not from jealousy but from the fear of lose the jewel of she time he unite the statesman the scholar and the soldier and as by the one he purchase fame and honor in he life so by the other he have acquire immortality after death in the year 1586 when that unfortunate stand be make against the spaniard before zutphen the 22d of September when he be get upon the third horse have have two slay under he before he be wound with a musket-shot out of the trench which break the bone of he thigh the horse he ride upon be rather furiously choleric than bravely proud so forced he to forsake the field but not he back as the noblest and fittest bier -lrb- say lord brook -rrb- to carry a martial commander to he grave in this progress pass along by the rest of the army where he uncle the -lrb- 3 -rrb- general be and be faint with excess of bleed he call for drink which be presently bring he but as he be put the bottle to he mouth he see a poor soldier carry along who have be wound at the same time wishfully cast up he eye at the bottle whereupon sir philip take it from he own mouth before he drink and deliver it to the poor man with these word thy necessity be yet greater than mine and when he have assist this poor soldier and fellow sufferer as he call he he be presently carry to arnheim where the principal surgeon of the camp attend he this generous behavior of we gallant knight ought not to pass without a panegyric all he deed of bravery he politeness he learning and courtly accomplishment do not reflect so much honor upon he as this one disinterested truly heroic action : it discover so tender and benevolent a nature a mind so fortify against pain a heart so overflow with generous sentiment to relieve in opposition to the violent call of he own necessity a poor man languish in the same distress before himself that as none can read it without the highest admiration of the wounded hero so none i hope will think I extravagant in thus endeavor to extol it bravery be often constitutional fame may be the motive to feat of arm a statesman and a courtier may act from interest but a sacrifice so generous as this can be make by none but those who be good as well as great who be noble-minded and gloriously compassionate like sidney when the surgeon begin to dress he wound he tell they that while he strength be yet entire he body free from a fever and he mind able to endure they might freely use they art cut and search to the bottom but if they should neglect they art and renew torment in the declination of nature they ignorance or over-tenderness would prove a kind of tyranny to they friend and reflect no honor upon themselves for some time they have great hope of he recovery and so zealous be they to promote it and overjoy at its seeming approach that they spread the report of it which soon reach london and diffuse the most general joy at court that ever be know at the same time count hollock be under the care of a most excellent surgeon for a wound in he throat by a musket shot yet he neglect he own extremity to save he friend and for that purpose send he to sir philip this surgeon notwithstanding out of love to he master return one day to dress he wound the count cheerfully ask he how sir philip do he answer with a dejected look that he be not well : at these word the count as have more sense of he friend 's wound than he own cry out away villain never see my face again till you bring better news of that gentleman 's recovery for whose redemption many such as i be happily lose find all the effort of the surgeon in vain he begin to put no more confidence in they skill and resign himself with heroic patience to he fate he call the minister to he who be all excellent man of different nation and before they make such a confession of christian faith as no book but the heart can truly and feelingly deliver then call for he will and settle he temporal affair the last scene of this tragedy be the parting between the two brother sir philip exert all he soul in endeavor to suppress he sorrow in which affection and nature be too powerful for he while the other demonstrate he tenderness by immoderate transport of grief a weakness which every tender breast will easily forgive who have ever feel the pang of parting from a brother and a brother of sir philip sidney 's worth demand still additional sorrow he take he leave with these admonish word my dear much loved honor brother love my memory cherish my friend they faith to I may assure you they be honest but above all govern you will and affection by the will and word of you creator in I behold the end of this world with all she vanity and with this farewel he desire the company to lead he away after he death which happen on the 16th of October the state of zealand become suitor to he majesty and he noble friend that they might have the honor of bury he body at the public expence of they government -lrb- 4 -rrb- but in this they be deny for soon after he body be bring to flushing and be embark with great solemnity on the 1st of November land at tower wharf on the 6th of the same month and the 16th of February follow after have lain in state it be magnificently deposit in st paul 's cathedral as the funeral of many prince have not exceed it in solemnity so few have equal it in the undissembled sorrow for he loss -lrb- 5 -rrb- king jame writ a epitaph upon he and the muse of oxford lament he compose elegy to he memory it may be justly say of of wit to estimate the merit of a piece as it happen to suit they appetite or encourage they in every irregular indulgence no man of honor who see a poet endowed with a large share of natural by loose writings but mr green must be free from the imputation of hypocrisy for we find he practice the very doctrine he teach winstanley relate that he be marry to a very fine and deserving lady to grow stale we find he fall into abject poverty and lament the life he have lead which bring he to it for it always happen that a mistress be a more expensive piece of furniniture than a wife and if the modern adulterer would speak the truth i be certain they would acknowledge that half the money which in the true sense of the word be misspend upon those daughter of destruction would keep a family with decency and maintain a wife with honor when we author be in this forlorn miserable state he writ a letter to he wife which mr winstanly have prefer and which as it have somewhat tender in it i shall insert it have often be observe that half the unhappy marriage in the world be more owe to the man than the woman that woman be in general much better being in the moral sense than the man who as they bustle less in life be generally unacquainted with those artifice and trick which be acquire by a knowledge of the world and that then they yoke-fellow need only be tender and indulgent to win they but i believe it may be generally allow that woman be the best or worst part of the human creation : none excel they in virtue but when they depart from it none exceed they in vice in the case of green we shall see by the letter he send he wife how much she be injure the remembrance of many wrong offer thee and thy unreproved virtue add greater sorrow to my miserable state than i can utter or thou conceive neither be it lessen by consideration of thy absence -lrb- tho ' shame would let I hardly behold thy face -rrb- but exceedingly aggravate for that i can not as i ought to thy ownself reconcile myself that thou might ` st witness my inward woe at this instant that have make thee a woful wife for so long a time but equal heaven have deny that comfort give at my last need like succor as i have seek all my life be in this extremity as void of help as thou hast be of hope reason would that after so long waste i should not send thee a child to bring thee charge but consider he be the fruit of thy womb in whose face regard not the father so much as thy own perfection : he be yet green and may grow strait if he be carefully tend otherwise apt enough to follow he father 's folly that i have offend thee highly i know that thou canst forget my injury i hardly believe yet i perswade myself that if thou sawest my wretched estate thou couldst not but lament it nay certainly i know thou wouldst all thy wrong muster themselves about I and every evil at once plague I for my contempt of god i be contemn of man for my swearing and forswear no man will believe I for my gluttony i suffer hunger for my drunkenness thirst for my adultery ulcerous sore thus god have cast I down that i might be humble and punish for example of other and though he suffer I in this world to perish without succor yet i trust in the world to come to find mercy by the merit of my savior to whom i commend thee and commit my soul thy repentant husband for he disloyalty robert green to sir walter raleigh call it a continue allegory or dark conceit he give we some remark on allegorical poetry in general define allegory to be a fable or story in which under imaginary person or thing be shadow some real action or instructive moral or as i think say he it be somewhere very shortly define by plutarch it be that in which one thing be related and another thing understand it be a kind of poetical picture or hieroglyphick which by its apt resemblance convey instruction to the mind by a analogy to the sens and so amuse the fancy while it inform the understanding every allegory have therefore two sens the literal and mystical the literal sense be like a dream or vision of which the mystical sense be the true meaning or interpretation this will be more clearly apprehend by consider that as a simile be a more extended metaphor so a allegory be a kind of continued simile or a assemblage of similitude draw out at full length the chief merit of this poem no doubt consist in that surprising vein of fabulous invention which run through it and enrich it every where with imagery and description more than we meet with in any other modern poem the author seem to be possess of a kind of poetical magic and the figure he call up to we view rise so thick upon we that we be at once pleased and distract with the exhaustless variety of they so that he fault may in a manner be the rest and tho ' some of the person make they appearance in different book yet this have very little effect in conceal they prince arthur be indeed the principal person and have therefore a share give he in every legend but he part be not considerable enough in any one of they he appear and vanish again like a spirit and we lose sight of he too soon to consider he as the hero of the poem these be the most obvious defect in the fable of the fairy queen the want of unity in the story make it difficult for the reader to carry it in he mind and distract too much he attention to the several part of it and indeed the whole frame of it would appear monstrous be it to be examine by the rule of epic poetry as they have be draw from the practice of homer and virgil but as it be plain the author never design it by these rule i think it ought rather to be call a poem of a particular kind describe in a series of allegorical adventure or episode the most note virtue and vice to compare it therefore with the model of antiquity would be like draw a parallel between the roman and gothic architecture in the first there be doubtless a more natural grandeur and simplicity in the latter we find great mixture of beauty and barbarism yet assist by the invention of a variety of inferior ornament and tho ' the former be more majestic in the whole the latter may be very surprizing and finished gentleman about court soon after he arrival in england he contract a intimacy which afterwards grow into friendship with sir robert carre a scotch gentleman a favorite with king jame and afterwards earl of somerset such be the warmth of friendship in which these two gentleman live that they be inseparable carre could enter into no scheme nor pursue any measure without the advice and concurrence of overbury nor could overbury enjoy any felicity but in the company of he he love they friendship be the subject of court-conversation and they genius seem so much alike that it be reasonable to suppose no breach could ever be produce between they but such it seem be the power of woman such the influence of beauty that even the sacred tie of friendship be break asunder by the magic energy of these superior charm carre fell in love with lady france howard daughter to the earl of suffolk and lately divorce from the earl of essex -lrb- 3 -rrb- he communicate he passion to he friend who be too penetrate not to know that no man could live with much comfort with a woman of the countess 's stamp of whose moral he have a bad opinion he insinuate to carre some suspicion and those well found against she honor he dissuade he with all the warmth of the sincerest friendship to desist from a match that would involve he in misery and not to suffer he passion for she beauty to king and to whom the imputation of impotence be lay the countess in order to procure a divorce from she husband give it out that tho ' she have be for some time in a married state she be yet a virgin and which it seem sit very uneasy upon she to prove this a jury of matron be to examine she and give they opinion whether she be or be not a virgin : this scrutiny the countess do not care to undergo and therefore entreat the favor that she might enter mask to save she blush this be grant she and she take care to have a young lady provide of much the same size and exterior appearance who personate she and the jury assert she to be a unviolated virgin this precaution in the countess no doubt diminish she character and be a circumstance not favourable to she honor for if she husband have be really impotent as she pretend she need not have be afraid of the search and it prove that she either injure she husband by falsely asperse he or that she have violate she honor with other man but which ever of these cause prevail have the countess be wise enough she have no occasion to fear the consequence of a scrutiny for if i be rightly inform a jury of old woman can no more judge accurately whether a woman have yield she virginity than they can by examine a dead body know of what distemper the deceased die but be that as it may the whole affair be unfavourable to she modesty it shew she a woman of irregular passion which poor sir thoma overbury dearly experience for even after the countess be happy in the embrace of the earl of somerset she could not forbear the persecution of he she procure that sir thoma should be nominate by the king to go ambassador to russia a destination she know would displease he it be then no better than a kind of honorable grave she
